RATING: 7
Modern Art is a pretty great auction game, but it's hard to get it to the table when [thing=12][/thing] and [thing=56786]Kamon[/thing] exist. Then again, those don't get to the table that much, either, so perhaps I just have too many auction games? 

In either case, I love the trickiness of the different types of auctions in this game. The simplicity of Ra is still a little more appealing to me, but this one seems to be easier to read your opponents for some reason. 

Plus, the latest edition by CMON is drop-dead gorgeous (so is the OINK games edition) so you're really spoiled for choice -- at such a low price, you really need to check this game out!

RATING: 8
An exploration of a single mechanic, this game is made up of five different variations on an auction process. As such it feels like a very pure game. Nothing distracts from its central theme. Auctions are, by their nature, interactive, so it never feels dry. The Mayfair English edition has terrible components (photocopied rules, bad artwork, horrible plastic poker chips) so I made my own set, which is much more satisfying to use.

RATING: 6
2008-07-10
Fun at first but after a while everybody has learned to play and is executing the same strategy.

2016-02-04
My initial comments were not fair, since I was playing with the same group. Change one person in the table and the dynamics of the game change completely. Sometimes you have to be aggressive and accept lesser profits just to win something. However, the basic strategy is still the same, spread the good you give evenly to the others while securing all good from others to yourself.

Modern Art is a solid auction game with no obvious flaws. The only downside of it is that you need at least 4 players for the bidding action to be any fun. The thing that keeps the game alive and interesting are the different auction mechanisms and market speculation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
First impressions are extremely good.

This is fun!  The players tend to fall around laughing at various points (I'm lucky to have a good crowd) as all sorts of jokes about the art and the worth of the artists fly around.

It's (almost) a heavy-medium brain-burner which makes itself feel lighter due to this banter and fun...that's a REALLY good quality in a game.

It has about the right element of luck in it.  Yes "Luck."  I think luck can really add subtlety and replayability.  There's enough here to sway the game a little, but not too much against strong players.

As I play it more, I would speculate that my score may move up a little.

RATING: 8
An auction game. Player sells art with different auction method. And player gets to buy arts as well. End of 1 season, sell the arts. Most art sold for that season value the most. Price of arts price accumulates after each season. And after 4 seasons, player with the most cash wins. 

Box comes with not-so-nice stuff but the idea of the game it's genius. It's fun, it's interactive. So grab a glass of water for everyone and start bidding.

RATING: 6
Neat auction game.  Would need to play again and see how the final scores are before making up my mind, though.

Gameplay seemed interesting, in that you can win without winning a single auction (in theory), but I'm not sure if that would hold up over time.

After another play, there seem to be pretty clear strategies about how to win, but the strategies aren't nearly as fun to use compared with just auctioning whenever you feel like it.  The game is also very susceptible to "not-so-great players decide the winner" effects, as one bid out of left field can decide who wins.

Similar to Dominion, playing for fun won't generally allow you to win, but playing to win won't generally allow you to have fun.

Would not need to own, but would probably play again.

RATING: 7
Very cool auction game, although not as straight-forward as Medici.

For a while, I thought I didn't know what a "good" bid was.  It seemed too simple of a mathematical calculation.  There had to be more to it.  But there isn't.  It is what it is, and the real "game" is in choosing to place the bid or not...NOT in determining what the right bid is.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fantastic. The new production by CMON is gorgeous. THis game plays quick but has interesting decisions every round. Very highly recommended.

for anyone playing this game for the first time: Buying paintings in this game is kind of a trap. It's easy to look at a painting that is going to be likely or even for sure worth $30 and say, if I bid $28, I'll make money so that's a good bid. But what just happened was the auctioneer made $28, and you made $2. If this type of bidding happens in your play, a seller will crush everyone. But in a really tight game, wooooo this is a razor's edge. Fantastic stuff

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
3-5(4,5)

A solid game that distills the gameplay to various types of auctions, and figuring out when to auction a particular artist, and which auction to use is brilliant, as is the fact that it's generally a poor idea to buy your own auction. Thus, there are a lot of interactions at play. You want to auction something appealing to others but also build up your own artists, and choose a maximal auction type, etc.

I agree with some that double auctions can feel a bit overpowered in that they can disproportionately influence the popular artists. The bidders are going to pump up the prices a bit, but the benefit really goes to the seller who gains from having the double. Still, a very engaging game.

RATING: 10
My lightbulb moment so far as Eurogames were concerned.  The rules are simple and yet Modern Art incorporates elements of hand management and timing with an absolute need to pay attention to what your opponents are doing and how they are bidding.  The auctions are deliciously tense.

I love this game enough to own both the German and the Chinese editions. The artwork on the Chinese cards is really nice, while the characters are almost as easily understood as icons.  The money is another story; the variations on brown and green are too difficult to tell apart.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Deep auction thinking I suppose.  But is it fun? Not that much.

For my tastes, the auctions feel a bit too tied up with mere financial calculations.  Crunching math for an hour or so is not THAT much fun...or is it?  Spreadsheets anyone?  And I suppose when you win, you get to feel like you calculated the value of more paintings, more accurately, more often, than the other players.  Hooray?! 

In addition, presumably, you "play your hand" in the correct order, which provides, at most, only moderate enjoyment for me in this game.  Which painting should I auction from my hand this round?  That's it.  That is your "interesting" decision every single blessed round.  Interesting? Barely.

Players who are dealt double auction cards (sometimes A LOT of double auction cards) seem to have advantages in the game, and the players who happen to immediately follow double auction dealt players also seem to have advantages, since suddenly the better market for that painter is virtually instantly established.  Does that break the game? Maybe. Does it really matter?  Maybe.

Players who foolishly bid too high, giving random players too much money for little worth, also break the game, or perhaps more accurately, DECIDE the game.

All in all, this game feels very close, but it is not quite, enjoyable.

After 4 plays the game still seems strategically fresh, but I'm not sure about its long-term ability to hold our attention once we've teased out most of the optimal values. 

As an interesting plus, the game is also a good learning tool for anyone interested in imperfect economic systems, price manipulation, or the applications of these concepts in the art market.

RATING: 8
The best auction game. The brazilian edition is a great looking one.

Very interesting mechanics where the key is to be the player who more closely identifies the potential value of an art work at the end of a season.

Also important is trying to keep track of which adversary is probably winning the game (or closer to you, if you think you're winning) and take care not to benefit this player too much when you trade with him, because on every purchase there's a division of profits between seller and buyer.

I think this dynamics is pretty neat, and makes for a very exciting game of bluffing and smart guessing. Not to mention the incredibly funny trash talk that goes around the table when players are trying to lower the value of art works put up for auction by their adversaries.

If you like auction games, you really should try this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's a great pure auction game. It allows players to shape the market and the skill--if you're playing it at least somewhat right--is in your ability to predict, control and shape the emerging market based on your hand of cards. Every little design decision helps shape the game, from the auctioneer being able to pay the bank and to the turn skipping that can occur due to double auctions. There's probably lots of depth to its hand management that can be wrung out of this game. The game, though, is a bit fragile. If a player isn't invested in trying to control the auction so certain artists are or aren't in the top 3 of sold artists, this game can go poorly. Still, this game has a great price point and a good production. It's probably best with 4 or 5 players to lessen the chance that one of the players is able to track the opposing players' money.

Not only would they be filling my coffers, theirs would be diminishing...that is if they weren't picking up the second or third piece of art that is in high demand.  I'll just have to keep playing and refine my bidding skills.  [Gift]

RATING: 8.1
Rarely has a game been brought back to it's bare essentials like this one. This is the ultimate bidding game. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Knizia hits another home run for me.  This game is loaded with a variety of strategies.  Knowing how much to pay, which paintings to sell, and how much to expect people to pay are all tough choices.  And there is a great build to the game as players gather more and more cash each season, but the bids become more and more educated.  I'm impressed by all the variety that can be found with just a few different styles of auctions.

But what really makes this game shine for me is the suggested variant rule that my brother instituted.  We played that you have to not only name the painting you are selling but also take on the role of a salesperson.  This lended a real party game atmosphere as we chuckled about the paintings and used ridiculous accents.  However, even with all this humor added it doesn't detract at all from the very strategic game that is underneath.

I still have hope it will bet better with player experience, but I haven't played a great game of it in awhile.

RATING: 10
Used to think that once everybody learns to bid optimally this game would stale. Now, I realize this isn't the case. When people start to bid optimally, you will then have to take that amount into consideration and adjust your game accordingly. This now turns into an incredibly good mind game: "Just how much is he going to deviate from the optimal bid?" 

People have complained that newbies could bid ridiculously, but I think this just adds a different layer of strategy where you would have do a little acting in order to entice or discourage newbies from bidding more. Excellent game.

RATING: 8
If every auction was the same style, I would really get bored with this game.  But the different auction styles really liven the game up and create a fun experience.  Although set collecting with auctions is not novel, the fact that the players decide which cards to offer for auction and the fact that previous' rounds winners can really impact scoring if they can be scored again adds something really unique to this game.

I have played the new version of this and do not like it as much.  I'd much rather play this older version with all the different auctions.

RATING: 10
The new CMON Edition of Modern Art is shockingly good. Their graphic design has been uneven in the past, but this is great - good usability, colorful, compact. Nice large, linen-finish cards. Excellent price point. And the contemporary artists are surprisgly effective. In general part of the satire of the theme is the terribleness of the art, and modern editions that have used actually good art makes the game attractive but sort of compromises the theme. The art in this edition - apparently by real contemporary artists - is in a good spot, obviously modern and edgy but colorful and more engaging than the checkerboards and spray paint of the classic pieces. It gives the game a more serious and less satirical flavor, but in a way that isn't inconsistent with the feel of the game.
---
This game really is an 11. If I could keep only one euro, this would probably be it.
---
Even after all this time - over 15 years of playing Modern Art - this game continues to amaze, impress, and inspire me. While I may have played games like Settlers more in that time frame, Modern Art is perhaps as close to a perfect "themed" game as I've ever played. Skill and luck are well-balanced, the pacing is perfect, there are elements of tactics, evaluation, and psychology, and it is brilliantly themed. The pieces it contains may be purposefully lousy, but the game itself is an exceptional work of art.
---
I'm not a terribly good Modern Art player, but I'll give  you my one tip that seems to have improved my play quite a bit: instead of trying to figure out what a piece is going to be worth, and then figuring out how much of a cut you are willing to give the auctioneer, instead try to think in terms of what economists call a "risk premium". Usually you can figure out what an artist probably will be worth, based on where it is currently. Then try to figure out what the risk is: what are the odds, based on your hand, the other players' holdings, where you are in the turn order, etc., that it will get trashed. Then discount your price based on your assessment of the risk. This will usually yield a sensible bid. The whole (expected value/number of players) thing is, in my opinion, bogus. You make money by taking risks on artists, and win by making good risk evaluations.

It should be said, Modern Art has a non-trivial luck element. The doubler auction cards are pretty powerful, and it sucks to not get them; and sometimes other players make unwise plays that screw things up (the "Puerto Rico" factor, although much less problematic here). But it's all thematic, and the game wouldn't work without the uncertainty.
---
A note on the '=' (double auction) cards: there are several variations on how the '=' cards should be played when used alone. The main contenders are the original German (player who matches runs the auction and takes all the proceeds) and the Mayfair (player who matches runs the auction and splits the proceeds with the original player). In both cases, play proceeds to the left of the player who matched. After playing both ways many times, I've decided I significantly prefer the original German. With the Mayfair matching rules, there is little reason not to play an unmatched '=' as a fairly nasty screw-you play, and the resultant turn-skipping can be brutal for the players who are skipped. There isn't really an ideal solution here, unfortunately. With the German variant, playing an unmatched '=' is so strongly discouraged (outside of end-of-round situations) that the arbitrary turn-skipping should never be an issue.
--
Updated notes on the different editions:
- Arte Moderna (Matagot): This is my current favorite incarnation of the game - hits a nice sweet spot with a colorful, pleasing, and unified graphic design that still plays to the somewhat satirical aesthetic of the game itself. An unusual set in that it has good-quality money chits in sufficient quantity. 
- Modern Art (lautapelit.fi): This is another excellent set, with the art cards again playing to the satirical modern art aesthetic. Plus I like the smaller box size. The surrounding details though (screens, board, box) isn't great and isn't as unified. The artists are also not as nicely distinct and two of the colors are pretty close. As a Modern Art fanatic I'm really happy to have this one but it's not as nice as the Matagot or Hans-im-Glück editions.
- Stamps: If you can get me a copy of this so I can comment on it, please let me know :)
- Wargames Hong Kong edition: If you're a Modern Art completist, obvious you want this. Duh. The Chinese aesthetic is great. But it's hard to use for non-Chinese readers, with Chinese characters instead of iconography and suit colors that are used very sparingly, making them hard to distinguish. The art doesn't seem particularly modern, but it's very nice. Another set I'm glad to have but don't break out all that often.
- Original German (Hans-im-Gluck): In some ways this is still the best edition of the game, with nice money, nice cards, and a nice, if oversized, box.
- Original Mayfair: The more sensible box size is a plus, even if the money is a touch cheesy. I ordered a second set of chips (Mayfair sells parts) to make for enough change.
- Small-box Mayfair: This is a great, compact game, but the card quality seems to have suffered. For the price, though, you can't beat it.
- Brazilian Arte Mordera: This is Mike Doyle's rework. The edition is aesthetically wonderful, although there are a number of usability issues: the great international symbols for auction type have been replaced by letters, which are not that meaningful outside of Portugese. Cash is a bit short and not double-sided. And the quality of the overall production does not match the quality of the presentation: cards are modest-quality and hard to shuffle or deal, and the screens are a bit flimsy. It's still a great novelty set, but I wish the same attention paid to aesthetics had been paid to the ergonomics as well. Also, it bears mentioning that the lousy art in the original is part of the theme. Replacing lousy art with some nice art does seem to (IMHO) make the theme a little less compelling.

I miss the days of simple, deep and elegant Euro's that could take one core mechanic (in the case of Modern Art, auctions) and squeeze every last drop of oomph out of the basic concept. I'm betting on the pendulum swinging back the other way as the power-gaming set realizes the hollowness of so many of the new-fangled Frankengames.

RATING: 7
Finally! A non-hideous version of a great auction game! True to my word all those years ago, I got a new version simply because it wasn't so hideous.

I always get the sense when playing Modern Art than it is cleverer than I will ever be. The way the value of items shifts in a way that's player driven, but guided by chance is fascinating. There's so many little things to consider on each turn - trying to set yourself up to end the round on YOUR terms, paying other players, bidding just to drive up the price for your opponents, etc. There's a lot going on in this game.

That said, I might enjoy thinking about the design of this game than I do actually playing it. Don't get me wrong - I thoroughly enjoy playing Modern Art, I just don't get the same kick from it that I do with Ra or Traumfabrik (or Dream Factory or whatever they decide to call it next). There's a little less angst, but possibly even more opportunity to feel incredibly clever.

RATING: 9
Seriously, how can this much quality gaming and so many potentially meaningful decisions be squeezed into such a short amount of time (45 min)? Surprising depth when played with experienced players. And yet it's easy to teach to newbies (even if they do throw the balance off for a few turns). 

My only gripe is the '=' cards, as their power determines so many games. We had experimenting with making sure everyone starts with an "=" card so that everyone has a chance for a bold play. Knowing everyone has at least one makes it a real question of timing big moves.

I'm not nuts about the production, but the game excels. The new version is soooo bleh. I love that the game is inexpensive, but it doesn't have to look cheap. Really, there needs to be a beautiful version of this game so that it stays a part of the gaming heirarchy. The design and raw auction goodness make this a classic on so many levels. I will not give this game the ten it deserves until the game is produced in the way it truly deserves.

When I look at the Japanese Stamps edition... I almost cry. So beautiful. And so out of reach.

RATING: 7
My criterion for rating games



http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1) Sense of wonder: Not much here as far as narrative or variability. You do see different cards and paintings each time, but that's about it. 

2) Meaningful/fun decisions.  This is a strength of the game. Lots of meaningful choices throughout the game mostly on how much to bid.

3) Player interaction: For me auction games are always a hit for player interaction. I like the back and forth of figuring out how much someone is willing to pay and dropping paintings I don't want on them but making them pay big for them.

4) Components/artwork: 1The cards and tokens are good quality, but the art is terrible. Oh, the irony.

5) Game length/complexity:  perfect length game. Just when the game is feeling tense, the game ends. I like that about this game. It is also not too complex. Most people can pick it up easy, but as the players all get better the challenge of the game goes up.

RATING: 5
Rated 8 after one play. What a blast! I almost gave this a nine... but I want to play it a few more times to see how this holds up. I absolutely love the hodge podge of various auctions in the same game. And, I was surprised at how thematic the game felt. Our group kept cracking jokes about the new "hot artist." 

I know the game gets accused of having a transparent end game where you can "do the math." But, I'm not sure that complaint is totally valid. For one thing, there are times when you need to start the auction... and that turn order has a value of its own that might not be possible to straight calculate. I'm not sure... a few more plays will tell. For now, I'm very enthusiastic to try this again.

Whoops: prior to a 2nd play, I realized we played this wrong. Auctions occur in turn order, not the person who won the prior auction. That makes a big difference. I really like this game, but with the 2nd play, I observed a love it/hate it dynamic. Some people struggled understanding how to value paintings, and the "strategy" was totally lost on them. I, however, find the game fascinating.

Update: A third play, and I'm dropping this to a 7, where I expect it will stay. The game encourages mathy calculating, and you're essentially bidding/betting on which artists other players will emphasize ("gee... bob has a lot of green. Will he try to end it?"). That's fine, but this surely is a pure auction game. It's good, and I like how the game brings in different auction types, but maybe I like "auctions in games" better than a pure auction game. I'll always want to play Ra over this.

Update: traded this away in a math trade. Loved the mix of auction types, loved the theme of trading wacky modern paintings, but I just couldn't get past the fact that it's too easy to start calculating the value of a lot. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Classic Knizia auction game where based on the card played that turn there can be 1 of 4 different auction types ranging from open bidding, closed reveal, once around the table, and set price. This game can be quite vicious with the auctions as that one painting you need could cost you a lot of money. A round ends when the 5th card is revealed for a particular artist and then the value of each artist is awarded based on which one has the most cards out that round. Sell your paintings and repeat that 3 more times and whoever has the most money wins! If you like auction games this is a great one, for me I'll stick with Isle of Skye or Biblios.

RATING: 9
Fantastic pure auction game. While the bidding currency is less interesting than Ra's (it's just the victory points) the different kinds of auctions and the decision of when to auction a certain piece, and what method is the best way to go is generally far from clear cut and makes this game very interesting. Do you try to finish the round now, or try to get greedy and make even more money in the process? Many small decisions add up to your final score. I would consider this very accessible given enough interest for casuals (short ruleset), but it definitely is heavy enough where an experienced player could mercilessly pound newbies at it (if they're any good at least, I can't win this game to save my life :) ), which means it has enough going on to be interesting to me.

This game is dependent on intelligent play, because the bidding system can get out of whack when someone is paying way too much for items (which then makes it a game of drawing the right cards as the person who sells the most pieces to that person is going to likely win...) but with the right group this is solid gold...coming up with the correct value for items is tricky, and the psychology of the auction in this game is second to none. I doubt a pure auction game will ever come along which does more things, in a more interesting way than this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I no longer own Modern Art because I stupidly gave it away. It is a wonderful, different game because it's the only game I've played with multiple types of auctions from which to choose. All you do is auction off paintings in the form of specialized colored cards, and the symbol on the card indicates the type of auction. You are auctioning off the painting to other players, so money changes hands among players rather than just the player and the bank. It's a great number-crunching, strategy game, but whatever you do, do NOT buy the card game version or the Masters Gallery or whatever other game related to Modern Art. STICK TO THIS ORIGINAL!

RATING: 1
Please consider this and believe in me before you are willing to have one.
This is an auction game with various method. In fact, no matter the method is, the bidding price is majorly depending on the valuation. Then it is important to add make the valuation not so certain but however the game mechanism fails. Turns out the bidding prices will go to some range that I can get benefit and the player playing the card is earning an amount of money not so different from me. In conclusion, a money splitting game. And there is no way to prevent the leading player to earn money when you want to earn too. How about forming an alliance by playing new cards to change the valuation? It is much more risky and earning easy safety money. Someone who first must earn less.
If you enjoy a long time, monotonic money splitting game, try this! Enjoy an game that you know you are losing due to some random stuffs and snowball not later than the half of the time.

RATING: 9
New version from CMON on my shelf (superb component quality), and my own custom-designed, print-and-play version as well (see https://boardgamegeek.com/article/25625985#25625985). 

Instead of five individual artists, I designed the art cards to feature artworks from the 16th–20th centuries available from the Met Museum's 350,000 free-to-use image library. I use black 8mm cubes to mark the end of round prices on a game board, and printed my cards with Drive Thru Cards on premium 320gsm black-core card stock.

RATING: 8.25
Most probably the best auctioning game ever.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A clever art auction game with beautiful components and an appealing playtime (~60 minutes) give its depth. The use of four different kinds of auctions within a single game and the appreciation of certain artists' work as the game progresses is absolutely inspired. The use of real artists and the designer's encouragement to actually "pitch" the art up for auction encourage a cultural dialogue that is (unfortunately) rare outside of a historical war game.

My primary complaint is that often the objective "value" of a piece of art up for auction is too obvious, which makes the auctions less dynamic, fails to reward risk-taking, and overly mathematical. I wonder what the game would look like if all art weren't sold until the very end of the game, rather than at the end of each round.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Starting to think this game is underrated. The rules are simple, playtime is short, and there is a healthy amount of strategy that doesn't come coupled with too much AP. And surprisingly for a Knizia game, the mechanics fit the theme quite well.

Seeing this relegated to below rank 100 (behind even an admittedly broken game like A Few Acres of Snow) is sure evidence that no one should care about BBG's rankings.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Brilliant auction game.

The balance of open and hidden information is superb - enough revealed for folk to make meaningful decisions with enough hidden information to provide both an exciting aura of suspense and a feeling of being 'in the know', whilst stopping it from being too dry and mathematical.

At its heart, it remains a mathematical game with a theme that could either be ignored or used for a side-serving of silliness.

As Ian pointed out, to widen a lead you must get 100% profit from a purchase - otherwise you give them more then you gain yourself. Thinking more, I now realise that if you buy from yourself, you're losing twice that amount. I think the same calculation could probably be made but it's even more important to not buy unless you make 100% profit.

I was definitely overvaluing the purchases.

Enjoyed the game though, despite losing. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Odysseia Jogos (Brazil) edition

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a good game, but the game isn't where people think it is. The game is not in getting deals. It's not in buying low and selling high. It's a game of what to sell and when. The auctions should provide the same basic result: as players become more skilled, they will tend toward optimal bidding strategies aimed at upping the price of art at the expense of other players' relative gain. That means that art is - generally - worth just under its probable sale price at auction. The profits of the auctioneer thus approach the deterministic.

Never buy your own painting, unless you are trying to drive up the price or it's insanely underpriced. The strategy lies in what to sell, not what you buy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The fun theme is enough to get me over my basic lack of excitement for all-auction games; this is then canceled out by the bizarre interpretation of the theme.  Art museums don't de-accession works to wheel and deal on the open market!!!  Sigh...

Anyway, it's well-structured and unfolds nicely, and the way artists accumulate value from turn to turn and round to round gives you enough variables to juggle that each auction is interesting to weigh out and gamble on.

RATING: 4
Awesome speculation economy sim.  Really fun trying to sell your auctions like a used car salesman.  Not a nine because of the double card.  You get money by being involved in deals for paintings.  People who are dealt Double cards are involved in more deals.  That's kinda unfair.

Update 7-4: Once you get thinking about the math you realize the game is cute but broken.  If I win that painting it is worth 0 because I can't close it out.  If you win that painting it is worth $200,000.  There is nothing I can do now or later to change that.  Just doesn't work as an economy game.

RATING: 7
[b]Tipo:[/b] Competitivo // [b]Interacción:[/b] Alta // [b]Azar:[/b] Bajo // [b]Rejugabilidad:[/b] Media
[b]Cartas:[/b] 70 de 66x97 mm
[b]Fundas recomendadas:[/b] Mayday "Premium" (tamaño 65x100mm)



[b]OPINIÓN PERSONAL:[/b]
Sé que mucha gente no lo considera como tal, pero si tuviese que elegir el mejor juego de iniciación escogería el Modern Art sin dudarlo: es fácil de explicar y jugar (después de todo, ¿quién no sabe lo que es una subasta?), sus partidas tienen una duración bastante discreta (50-60 min) y el tema no desagradará absolutamente a nadie (venta de cuadros... creo que es uno de los temas más serios y no-frikis que he visto).

Pero a parte de poder sacárselo a cualquiera, el Modern Art tiene la virtud de ser más profundo de lo que parece a simple vista: si eres un hacha de la economía quizás sepas pillar todas sus estrategias en la primera partida, pero lo normal es que vayas descubriéndolas según juegas (por algo es de Reiner Knizia).

En resumen, es un juego tremendamente disfrutable, con menos azar del que sugiere (en el fondo no importa tanto los cuadros que te toquen, sino el provecho que sepas sacarles), y con una rejugabilidad bastante aceptable (a ver, si lo sacas todos los días lo acabarás quemando, pero es de estos que te puede apetecer echar "la revancha").

RATING: 8.5
I really enjoy the game mechanics in Modern Art. There are lots of different possible auctions. Not everybody I know likes this game. I guess it depends if your into auctions or not. It's simple, and doesn`t have a lot of game material.

The more the game advances, some paintings of same artist can be worth a lot to some sellers for a nice profit. I find it to be quite an intense game in the 3rd and 4th round of the game. The more intense it gets, the more I enjoy playing Modern Art.

Having good judgement from how much you want to spend when buying a painting and how much you are willing to depart one of your own paintings can be critial between a 1st or 2nd place. You the look at each painting is worth in every round.

I've been on a winning streak at this game lately. In my last game I won, in the second year, I manage to get zero income from buying other players paintings. I guess I wasn't a big buyer and more of a seller in that game. Sell paintings at high cost and buy them cheap as possible to win!

quite simply .. a gem !!!  ... wonderful auction mechanics, with five differing auctions to buy and sell paintings ... a wonderful game -- easy to teach and learn, and easy to play -- the artwork features five aspiring artistes -- great fun , and strategy -- shall I end the round , ohh I need more money yet, what to sell, and shall it be a silent auction, or a raucous free-for-all open auction -- my only quibble with the game:: not enough tokens for coins (and what no tens!!??!!) .. this, of course, can be remedied by obtaining your own poker chips or adding more chips (tenners) to the game -- otherwise, an absolute winner -- solid entertainment, always always willing to play this one !

RATING: 9
Another fantastic Knizia game. 

This game is pure player interaction - how could it not be? It's just auctions all the way through. But what is really interesting is how there is deep strategy embedded in the decisions of which art pieces to auction off at which point. 

Like many other Knizia games, this one also has the player screens to keep your cards and money tokens hidden from other players. This small piece of cardboard has been the source of many dramatic moments - one more than one occasion, the winner ended up being someone that the rest of us did not expect. The game has dramatic finishes in spades.

RATING: 6
If you like auctions, then this is the game for you!  Not only are there auctions, but there are multiple types of auctions, such as blind-bidding, once around the table, free form, and selling things at a set price.  I find it interesting to watch as different artists get more and more valuable as many of them become sold.  The strategy wasn't easy for me on the first game and there is a lot of planning based on what you have in your hand.  I still think that Ra is Knizia's quintessential auction game but this one is really worth trying as well.  It's a shame that the best looking editions of this game aren't released in North America. Spiteful/newbie players can knowingly/unknowingly hand the game to other players. While I haven't really experienced it, it's certainly worth mentioning.

RATING: 8
Pitch-perfect combination of math, economics, and psychology.  And a great simulation of the theme.

I played a game where three of five had no idea what they were doing and made ridiculous bids.  Rather than ruin the game, it just enhanced it - it was the playing out of an illogically overheated art market, and it was fascinating for the two of us to figure out how best to take advantage of that market.

RATING: 5
Multiple types of auctions and items of uncertain value; to me this was a very different game to what I'm used to.

Duration: This took a while, but there were always choices and bids to make.
Design: Simple and straightforward rules and mechanics, but lots of depth, especially with many players and hidden money.
Strategy: There's one level of strategy in deciding whether to purchase something, and another in trying to put out works for auction.
Components: Decent cards and counters.
Theme: On one level this is an abstract, but I thought it fit well.

RATING: 7
[Auction, Art, Prisoner's Dilemma] {5P:7, 4P:8, 3P:4} A deliciously complex bidding game, as one might expect from THE designer with a doctorate in mathematics, RK. The varying auction modes are maddening. Will clearly get better with more plays. *** Update: Now that I finally own this sucker, and am getting to play it more, it's clearly better than initially thought. Rating up to 8. Thanks for the reprint, Mayfair! *** UPDATE: Ok, this is now a nine, partly because I can't figure it out, partly because I can't win, and partly because I truly believe I should be able to figure it out and yet can't. Welcome to the demigods, Modern Art, I'm glad I go to know you this intimately. Now in the same exclusive class as Taj Mahal - "Games Fawkes Sucks At But Loves Anyway" *** UPDATE: Now I understand you Modern Art, and I understand your greatness. You don't feel like a 10, because there's not enough going on, but for a non-heavy game, I think you're as good as it ever gets. 9.5, and if you're still played as heavily in a year as you are now, then I'll be able to make you a 10. *** UPDATE: Back to 8.5, burning out (might not be the game's fault tho). Or rather, I think I'll now settle on an 8. MA is an exercise in Game Theory with incomplete information, in an almost-pure form. My group is playing very, very loose, and is creating runaway winners. The lack of depth shows in this case, as there are no other roads to victory, and no way to mitigate the looseness of the bids. *** UPDATE: Well, Modern Art's weakness is found in the 'weak/greedy player syndrome' that screws games. I think that part of my frustration is that I can see the game theory effects clearly as the game is going, and am frustrated by loose play. Modern Art remains an excellent game, but the frustration takes its toll. Am still glad I own it, and would still recommend it to anyone. If you know any game theory, keep your cool. =P ***UPDATE: Modern Art doesn't work well with 3P. It's ok with 5, but like many other German games it's best with 4 due to the added control.

RATING: 6
My first two plays were asterisk games, since we didn't realize that the fifth card auctioned counted towards the number of paintings sold.  That changed the dynamic a lot!  I really like this game, and I suspect there is more to learn.  Simple rules, but a lot of depth: this game could follow Acquire as one of my favorites.  

The path to victory is clearer in this game than Acquire.  Acquire has more finesse, whereas this game feels more like wrestling.  There seems to be more control in Modern Art than in most other games.  Much of that is due to the length of play.  It clocks at 45 minutes, but I suspect that players who are familiar with it could play faster.

But after three plays, what do I know?

RATING: 7.1
Não é o meu gênero mas um é ótimo jogo de leilão. Nós somos leiloeiro de grandes museus do mundo e temos em nossas mãos obras de arte para leiloar. Estas obras caso sejam postas em leilão podem ser adquiridas por qualquer jogador inclusive o próprio leiloeiro. O jogo ocorre em quatro temporadas (turnos). São leiloadas várias obras de modo que o 1º, 2º e 3º artista mais popular, ao final de cada uma das temporadas, tem suas obras avaliadas em 30, 20 ou 10 mil, respectivamente, e quem adquiriu obras destes três artistas se capitalizam para as temporadas futuras. O interessante é que você pode adquirir obras que não estarão entre as três mais populares, por tanto não terá valor algum naquela temporada, e obras que estarão entre as três mais populares e estarão valorizadas. Esta valorização é acumulativa. O ponto alto do jogo são os 5 tipos de leilão, aberto, fechado, lance único, preço fixo e duplo, que dão uma dinâmica bem divertida ao jogo. O dinheiro adquirido no final das temporadas e na venda das obras de arte define quem vence. Faço questão de destacar a qualidade da versão brasileira, da Odysseia jogos. A qualidade dos materiais, a arte, a caixa, o manual e o certificado de autenticidade da edição com nº da unidade dão uma aula de como produzir um jogo. É perfeita. Recomendo!

RATING: 7
It's good...stripped down auction game with a variety of auctions.  Although I hear people say "this is better than Ra", I still don't see it.  I could see someone possibly liking this as much as Ra, but not saying this is absolutely "better".  Ra is an auction game stripped to its most elemental; this is a bridge too far in that despite its additional auction types, this is stripped down TOO far, and the bones show.  You manipulate stuff by playing cards from hand, trying to gauge prices.  It just feels more like a gamey little card game.  A good one...but I know Ra, and you, sir, are no Ra.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.9
Finally played it!  Though after only a game and a half (the first game we had to restart because we goofed), I love it!  It could be my new favorite game!  Far more depth than meets the eye!  Not only do you have to worry about owning highly sought after art each round, you also have to be concerned about who is making all the profit before the "profit taking" phase each round!  I can see those who has photographic memory with regards to math would have an "unfair" advantage in this game...  But hey, it's fun!  It's fairly light, and very easy to learn and to teach.  Highly recommend it!  BTW, this game I find to be far more enjoyable than Ra!  It's for the simple fact that I find it not nearly as frustrating each round, and because the results are mostly hidden, it gives you a sense that you are still in the game the whole time. 

Finally won my first game after 3 tries!  Rating drops a tad because the urgency to play this again is slightly lower...

I have in my collection:  

1x German Version Sealed/Mint

RATING: 9
Without question, one of the classics of the auction genre. It's a game that initially appeared to me to be rather straightforward, but experience revealed an amazing amount of depth and subtlety.  Modern Art is a great example of Knizia's underrated ability to convey the correct theme within a sparse, abstract game design (other examples include Euphrat & Tigris, En Garde, LoTR: the Confrontation, and Royal Turf).  I prefer to play with a variant that evenly distributes the "double" cards.  (For example, with four players, everyone gets two doubles in the opening hand and one more in the second batch of cards.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is a game that reveals itself in a subtle way, and for that reason I find it hard to teach to people. I managed to get my mother to play, thinking it would be a great game for after Sunday lunch. She was fine, but my dad balked, which is frustrating, because he loves a good auction. The buying and selling of the paintings at the right times for the right amounts, becomes a real skill, negating much of the luck factor. The main luck being what cards you are initially dealt. But even then you can play to the strengths of that hand. There is a definite learning curve in that respect, making it pretty much impossible for a newbie to beat an experienced player. Having said that, my mother kept winning because she would end auction rounds quickly, as soon as she saw she would make the most profit. That's too easy to do. I think the true spirit of the game is found by cautiously buying and selling as many paintings as possible and ending it at a tactical point whereby you make as much profit as possible and not too much for everybody else, whilst keeping an eye on your cards for the next round. Ending all the rounds quickly the moment you see your advantage makes for a quick, but unsatisfying game I believe. We play this with 4 in under 45 minutes, if mom isn't playing! 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A masterclass in auctions by Reiner Knizia. The game uses four different types of auctions and the choice of when to use them is the key to success.
The rules seem fairly simple but they really open the game for the players to make interesting choices. 
The beating heart of this game is not the auctions though, but the scoring system. It's really clever how the artists' popularity changes the painting's value, and how they change over time.
By far the best auction game I've ever played.

RATING: 7
After playing, I realized that I think this is secretly a negotiation game: if I am auctioning a piece that will be worth 80, then those 80 points will be split between me and the high bidder and the only question is how we split them.

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
Modern Art is an excellent, but fragile auction game. The quality of the game will depend on each person's choices. At first, it is difficult to determine when to set the market, bluff investments, or end the round. Unfortunately, a poor decision here can affect the market or end the round with no real benefit.

For what the game does well, Modern Art has an engaging market mechanic. It sets itself apart from other auction games because you get to determine the value of your artwork collected. This is a deep and rewarding game that reveals itself after multiple plays.

RATING: 5
This was fun, but didn't live up to its hype as a great auction game.  The fact that it require 4-5 players hurts it too.  The different auction types and all the symbols are a little off-putting to casual gamers.  I felt like it was For Sale with crap added that didn't make the game any better.  The components disappointed me a little bit and the game was too dry for how long it was.  The system for determining how much the paintings were worth was very good, and the dynamics whereby you can end the round add tension and some interesting decisions.  I felt that buying up as many of the "winning" artist's paintings as possible was the best strategy, even if they were bought for expected value minus one.  I think my mistake was, I was always trying to close the auction once I had done that, when I should have been selling paintings instead.  I definitely want to play this again because I think I would do better and see more of the meat of the game on a second play. 

 I'd like to play it again, but I don't think I'll ever really like it.  The different auctions are too complicated to play with non-gamers and if I am going to play a gamer's game, there are a lot of other games I'd rather play.

RATING: 9
This is an auction game for people who really love auction games. It's simple to grasp, but the implications of the choices you make are extremely deep and subtle. Not only will you be required to place bids (along with the valuation skill that requires), you will make choices about what TYPE of auction to initiate, with the turn order sometimes making a big difference. As the game progresses, it becomes clearer which artists will grant the biggest payoffs, meaning that the start of the game gives a bit more wiggle-room for errors as the players get a feel for it. There are lots of subtle skills that only become clear with several plays. A really enjoyable experience.

RATING: 9
This game is very intuitively obvious even to complete newbies to gaming, but it's quite deep and has lots of replayability.  Prices of art are somewhat based on groupthink, so you have to adjust your strategy to the group that you're playing with.

Some say that the "right" price for a painting is about 2/3 of its value.  That is, if the painting will be worth $30 at the end of the round, you should pay $20, because then you lose $10 (net) to the seller, but make $10 on everybody else.  Of course, you never know what the true value of a painting is until the round is over (although that if 4 Lite Metals have been auctioned in a round, they will place at least second in that round).

It's clear that you can make more money by buying paintings than by selling them.  Remember -- you only get one chance to sell every 3-5 turns, but you could make money during many turns by buying paintings at a discount.

Someone once gave me the warning that there is a high correlation between the number of double-sale ("=") cards that you are dealt and your chance of winning.  I don't know what strategy to follow when you only have zero or one of these "=" cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[Initial Impression: 9]
Perhaps the purest auction game around.  Players take the role of art dealers, selling paintings of up-and-coming artists and buying paintings from other dealers to fill their galleries.  Good play requires not only a calculation of expected value, but also bluffing, assessing the strategy of other players, and acting as an effective auctioneer.  With many types of auctions, the action is never dull and tension is high right to the end-game.  It holds high replay value, mainly because of the large psychological component - reacting to the style of your opponents is far more important than playing a set optimized strategy.  I love that it is possible to win without ever buying a single painting!  Play the original German rules (the English "Double Trouble" variant) to mitigate some of the power of double auctions.

RATING: 9
A modern classic and one of my favorite auction games. Where this stands out from others in the genre are the multiple kinds of auctions (Dutch, silent, once-around, etc) that add a layer of variety. The scoring is simple and nifty -- nothing has inherent value. It is a stark contrast to so many "economic" games where some invisible hand has set the prices of everything in the world.

RATING: 9
One of only three games I own which I recommend to both family and gamers, the others being Acquire and Scotland Yard.  The beauty of this game is that money for paintings being bought/sold stays amongst the players, so the benefit of every purchase must be weighed against the disadvantage of enriching the seller.  The game fuels great social interaction too.

I am tempted to nominate it "the purest auction game", but stop short only because I feel I must play a few more before concluding that!  In the mean time, I will declare it "my favourite pure auction game"!

RATING: 9
The perfect auction game.  Just when you think you have it figured out, there is another layer revealed.  Absolutely incredible.  I thought I had a good plan with the bidding.  Just bid a little over half of the anticipated final value of the piece of art.  The problem is that the two people involved in the transaction make a profit and the other players receive nothing.  Is it better to pay more than half plus a bit to be one of the players making a profit - any profit - vs one of the players who receive nothing?  Is it better to win your own auctions at any price below the anticipated final value to deprive the other players of any profit?  Maybe selectively outbid the player who is a threat and let the player who isn't a threat win it to make more of a profit.  My mind is spinning.  A game shouldn't be easily figured out.  Is it even possible to figure this one out?  This game is just great!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Last Played: 2010-2015
Plays: 1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
遊戲設置 Set up

每人發100圓作為開始的資金。把牌洗好後接著依照遊戲人數發給每個人不同張數的手牌，5人玩發8張、4人玩發9張、3人玩發11張，其餘的面朝下作為牌庫。
玩者的錢是隱密的(屏風就是來擋錢的)，也不能將手上的牌公開。
牌張數由左到右12/13/14/15/16

遊戲流程 Playing the Game

隨機決定一個玩家先開始，從他開始順時針輪流賣畫。 
輪到一個人賣畫時，他必需要從手上選一幅畫來賣，如果他手上只有一張牌就必須打出那一張。賣的時候由出牌者來當主持人，成交的畫作公開放到買主面前，交易過的畫不得回到手上，也不得再拍賣。

回合結束的條件是，當有任何一個畫家的畫出現了五張時立即結束。
注意：第五張畫本身不得拍賣、也不屬於任何人(即使是主持人)，它唯一的作用是將回合結束掉。另外如果第五張是Double的話則不需找第二張搭配。

Open -- 公開競價
喊價者必需喊出比前一位喊價者的價錢高；主持人自己也能參與喊價。
如果都沒有人喊價，主持人便以零元自己買下。
Round (Once around) -- 一回合競標
由主持人左邊的人開始喊價，順時針繞一圈。
輪到某一個人喊價時，他可以選擇棄權，也可以喊一個價，但條件是必須是比上一家所喊的價高，最後一個喊價的是主持人自己，他能自己喊一個價買下，也能棄權，把畫賣給最後一個喊價者。

Sealed -- 秘密喊價 (暗標)
包括主持人從手上拿出若干數量的錢，抓在手上，同時亮出，由最高價者得標。
如果最高價由兩個以上的人開出，則由主持人左手邊最近的那一個人得標。
除非主持人也是出最高價的人之一，這種情況則由主持人得標。

Fixed -- 定價拋售
主持人出一個定價，這個價必須是主持人手上的錢所買得起的。
主持人左手邊的那一個人先說要不要以這個價錢買下，如果要的話就付錢買話，
不要的話則再以順時針問下一家要不要買。
如果都沒有人接受所定的價錢的話，主持人必須自己出錢把畫買下。

Double -- 成套賣畫
Double的畫有兩種賣法：
1.主持人拿出一張Double，再配上另一張同畫家但非Double的畫作，兩張一起賣。賣法則依第二張的種類。
2.主持人拿出一張Double，問他左邊的人願不願意參加。
要參加的話就必須打出一張同畫家但非Double的畫作。如果左邊的人不願或不能參加，如果一圈下來都沒人願意的話，則主持人能免費得到那一張畫。
如果有願意參加的話，那個人就變成新的主持人，以第二張畫的賣法賣畫。
賣得的錢由兩個人對分，奇數的話，多出的一元由新的主持人得到。
如果是舊的主持人買下則付一半給銀行、一半給新主持人，奇數多出的一元給新主持人。
新主持人買下的話也是一樣，祇是要把奇數多出的一元給銀行。
####注意：兩人合作賣出兩張畫後，下一個由新主持人左邊的人開始賣畫，中間的人全被跳過！###

第一回合結束
開始結算畫價。
將一個30元的標碼放在五張畫作的那一個畫家名字之下的第一個格子上(一共有四格代表四個回合)，
出現畫數第二多的則擺20元的標碼，第三多則擺10元。
其餘名次的畫家沒有價值。如果有同樣數量的畫家，則以名字在棋盤最左邊者優先。
###所有的人必須立刻將面前的(不包括手上的)畫依標碼的價格賣給銀行，賣出的畫放到美術館不得再使用。
###結束回合的那一張或兩張(是Double的話)畫不得賣出，直接進美術館。
下一回合開始
###補牌，用牌庫的牌來補，而不是美術館裡的。五個人玩的話，每一個人補三張；四個人補四張；三個人補六張。
####只有第二、三回合有補牌，第四回合不再補牌。
由上一回合結束遊戲者的左邊那一個人先開始。

當第四回合結束錢最多的人便是贏家。

RATING: 10
Sencillamente, el mejor juego de subastas que he probado. En mi opinión, lo tiene todo: diversión, tensión, estrategia, astucia y mucha interacción... Perfecto. Me sorprende que otros juegos muy inferiores, como "Se vende", gocen de una calificación similar en la BGG. El 10 es porque, sencillamente, es el mejor juego que conozco en su género.
En el juego somos marchantes de arte. El objetivo es ser el que más dinero gane. Para ello, venderemos los cuadros que poseemos y compraremos otros. La clave está en saber manejar el mercado, compuesto por las obras de cinco artistas. A la hora de vender, cada obra establece el modo en qué se hará: subasta, subasta a ciegas, subasta a una ronda, precio fijo o subasta doble. El juego finaliza al cabo de cuatro rondas.
El juego se puede encontrar a un precio aceptable en el mercado nuevo o de 2ª mano, pero las cartas son horrorosas... imagino que para no pagar derechos de autor. Esto me convenció para fabricar mi propia versión con auténticas obras de arte. Siempre lo juego con mis hijos y me parecía un desperdicio renunciar al componente divulgador que un juego como éste puede tener. Así que no tienes excusa para no hacerte con él, bien comprándolo o bien imprimiendo alguno de los P&P que pueden hallarse en internet.

RATING: 9
Knizia's finest hour. An apparently simple bidding game which involves estimating the value of a commodity at the end of a round, then deciding how much 'cut' you are are prepared to give the auctioneer given your ability to boost the value during your turn. The game's not just about mathematics though, you need to listen to the 'market' and work out what people will pay over the odds for, then there's some luck (the '=' cards are very powerful and it hurts if you're not dealt them). There's also a chaos factor as you don't get anywhere without buying, but some players get caught up in bidding too much. This calculation of expected value can make the game borderline unplayable in some groups as the bidding tends towards the ultimate value of the painting and then the winner is the player selling the most (i.e. the one playing the most '=' cards). However, this is always enjoyable, has a (seemingly) short learning curve and for once in a Knizia game the theme fits perfectly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4.9

I really need to play this with a larger group.  It's a really neat application of the prisoner's dilemma.  Only the seller and buyer are going to profit, so as the buyer you should be willing to give the seller most of the profit just so he doesn't share the profit with another potential buyer.

Aug08: Ended up tossing it.  Auctions are fun, but not if you can't value the item.  Newbies tend to through the game to whoever offers up the doublers.  I'll stick to Ra in the mean time.

RATING: 9
The non-linear valuation of paintings makes this a solid '10' in game play. I love the fact that every painting has a different value to each player depending on who buys it, each player's overall position relative to each other player, what cards are in each player's hand, and so on. Really fun mental calculations can be had on almost every auction in this game.

However, the component quality is truly dreadful. The plastic money chips are cheap looking, feeling, and are missing an important denomination. The cardboard screens aren't much better. Due to the components this game cannot be rated a '10'.

RATING: 10
This is one of my top 10 games of all time, and definitely my favorite auction game -- largely because it's as much about the cardplay and reading the other players (and trying to manipulate them) as much as it is about the auctions. 

I also love love love how simple the rules are for such a very deep game. I've actually had great success playing it with nongamers -- sure, they'll play suboptimally/unpredictably and so you can't anticipate and manipulate the proceedings as much as you can with gamers, but the open auctions can be really fun, especially if you play with house rules that (1) you have to give a name to each painting you put up for auction and (2) you have to talk in the stereotypical accent of the city name on your player screen. It's Reiner Knizia's best party game! (seriously)

RATING: 8
A fairly light playing, fun and fast pace auction game with a short playing time as well making it very accessible to just about any group. Playing well however will take many games as the strategies are quite subtle here. 

This feels like a card game cleverly disguised as an auction game (even though it is a pure auction game), and how clever it is. The best part of the game is the psychology behind the scenes and the laughter that comes from it. There is certainly some nice strategic play to be had here and timing plays a critical role in it. 

Funny when I first heard about this game I thought it would be more like the old classic "Masterpiece" with paintings, psychology, auctions and the like but this is very very different in a good way. Simple rules and variety in the type of auctions big positives here, great little game that is fun every time.

RATING: 9.5
Custom deck made at artscow.com.  This is one of those games that the fun level depends on who you play with.  It can be maddening to play with newbies that haven't grasped what a reasonable bid is, and don't understand how they are helping or hurting other players.  Getting a bad hand can also ruin the game, but when you have a decent hand and are playing with experienced players, this game is amazing.

RATING: 8
I love this game. Knizia's Top 5 along with Taj Mahal, Tigris & Euphrates, Medici and Ra. The auction can be quite brutal as players conspire with one another to raise or drop the price of an artwork. Mechanically, this game combines all kinds of auctions found in most Euro games (open bid, one round bid, closed bid, name a price). The game is also an entry game since the rules are so simple, yet the player interactions make up the game. Brilliant.

Initial Rating: 8.0 (January 2011)

One of the best Knizia´s I have ever played, I love auction mechanics and this game has a lot of them. The game is simple enough to teach in 5 minutes but it has a surprising depth, sadly I have not played it as much as I want to. IMO one of the best (if not THE best) auction game out there (although I have not played Ra or Medici.)

The art of the game is adequate but it could have been a lot better, however it doesn’t detract from the fun of the game.

This game is a must have in any game collection.

[hr]
2017-02-20

I continue to like Modern Art but my desire to play it again is decreasing over time. One of the issues I have with the game is that it is too fragile. When a player pays too much for a work of art, he unbalances the game and that opens the possibility for a win, even if the player played a sub-optimal strategy. I dislike this as I feel it isn’t a deserved win. If a player played better he should win in an strategy game, but that’s not the case here.

Another issue I have with Modern Art is when players purposely  generate king-making situations. I have seen a player paid almost all his money to make another player win :/. There is no way to control tis with the rules, so I only like to play with very competitive players, players who won’t throw the game.

I still like the game though.

Current Rating: 7.0
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
(This comment is only for the Modern Art CMON edition 2017, to read the full comment see my other entry of the game)

2017-08-31

Initial Rating: 7.0 (August 2017)

I´m very glad CMON decided to reprint this classic. I love Modern Art and it was a very expensive game.

The rules of the game have changed a bit and I dislike the new rule, it feels unfair. When a player offers a double bid he can allow the next opponent to add a painting of the same kind. Under the old rules both players would share the profits by half. Under the new rule the opponent will get 100% of the profits. It is unfair as each player supplied a painting. In all honesty this change could have been made before the CMON edition, but I only noticed it now. I checked the rules I have and each player wins money if they played a painting.  It is a minor issue though, but it bothers me a bit.

Another minus for me is that the players shield doesn’t have the aid that they used to have. That was very useful to explain the game and to move the game forward in a fast manner. In our game many players had to ask again ¿what kind of bid is this? A minor issue, but I don’t know why they were removed.

The game´s components are ok and functional but I vastly prefer the poker chips of the older edition.

From my limited perspective, the art is now amazing. In the previous edition it was ugly, now it is gorgeous and the cards are Dixit big, so you can appreciate it a lot better. A friend said the art is very cool but the graphic design was bad. I didn’t understand why he said that (he is an expert on the topic) until I realized the paintings have names. It was only until the end of the game that I noticed this and my friend told me that was due to bad graphic design. He also told me more examples that I didn’t really understand so I won’t write them here :D.

Bottom line, I´m very glad Modern Art is available now. I won’t buy it because I have the old ugly edition, and that's enough for me. If a game looks great, it is a plus, but I´m always more concerned about gameplay than looks and the game plays great. I totally recommend it. For the full comments on the game, please read the other entry.

Current Rating: 7.0

Our position on games focussing on auction mechanics is shifting to less enjoyment.

I haven't seen any other game like this one. An "abstract bidding" game? I'd rate it higher if I specifically enjoyed the art-world spoof element as well as the game mechanics. As it stands this is pure distilled essence-of-auction. The items you bid for don't let you take some funky action later, or give you extra resources toward some build. The things you buy just... sit there... and, um, change value.

RATING: 8
Simpler mechanisms and more fun than most auction games, although the variety of auction types gives it an edge. However, the game's made by the subtle underplay between the players as they try to figure out what each painting is likely to be worth given their hands and how they read the other players' intentions and holdings.

Classic Knizia; super-simple mechanics belying a remarkable amount of psychological interplay between the players. No other designer does this stuff better - right up my street!

RATING: 7
An open bidding auction for a painting of a known value could logically produce a set of marginal decisions that do not reach equilibrium until the known value is approached.  However, the game is saved by the different types of auction mechanics and the value of a painting is never known with full certainty until the round ends and will also depend on the cards of the players.

RATING: 9
One of the best auction games, maybe THE best for 5 players. The problem is that the gameplay suffers significantly with fewer players, with the score dropping by as much as 2 points for every player you lose. So while the game is not very versatile, I think it absolutely sings with 5. It's very easy to get caught up in the auctions and overpay like crazy. Keep in mind that you'll often make more money selling a painting that you put up for auction than buying it yourself. A disciplined approach is required to win, I think.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is an interesting auction game which has different types of auctions depending on the card. You start with a hand of cards that have paintings by various artists. After five from one artist are sold the round ends. Then the three artists with the most art sold that round give you points for most to least sold of 30, 20, or 10 coins. After four rounds the player with the most money wins. Having the different auction types adds to the fun of the game.

RATING: 8
This is commonly hailed as Knizia's purest auction game and it also highlights what he does best - a simple ruleset that gives rise to many interesting angles that you don't immediately think of. Rating has climbed steadily the more I play it.

I held out for French version by Matagot rather than picking up the English version by Mayfair. Absolutely recommended, the better components and much superior style really do add to my enjoyment of the game. And it's a game that you can teach to newbies, so visual appeal is helpful.

RATING: 9
Wow, what a tremendous game.  After reading the rules one would think that this is simply a "greatest hits" auction game, but it's so much more than that.  The way that the paintings fluctuate wildly in value is a lot of fun, seems very thematic, and provides a roadblock to those who would just try to calculate the value.  It boggles my mind to think of how this game was designed - the different styles of auctions mesh together quite nicely and add to the turn angst.  The cumulative effect of each round keeps interest high, right up till the end.  What's more, it plays great with three players.  I've had pretty good luck with Knizia auction games with 3 players, but this one surprised me.  Medici is pure and elegant, Traumfabrik is very thematic, and I still can't put my finger on Ra.  But to me this one rises above its peers, which is really saying something when you consider the company.  My one caveat is that the fun factor definitely depends on the types of players.

RATING: 8
Rating for Brazilian version. 

Pros: Artwork is really nice. I guess part of the fun of Modern Art is the ridiculous artwork (and artist names), but it's really cool to look at these cards.

Cons: The biggest annoyance is that the money chits are not double sided, causing extra confusion and down time in keeping all of your chits right side up.  Also, the fact that there are sprues still left on the cards is a little annoying as well.  Finally (and this is not the game's fault), using letters to distinguish the different kinds of auctions as opposed to symbols is an annoyance.

So, while Modern Art is one of my favorite games, the presentation is nice, but the components leave you wishing that they had just invested a little more effort.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very clever mathematical representation of the skyrocketing and crashing prices for modern art (Karl Gitter and Yoko and the white painting in the play "Art" really are interchangeable). As an economist I can appreciate its incorporation of risk, expected value, and an intuitive demonstration of stock options. It also shows how easy it is to manipulate prices in a thin market. This is a wonderful game for use in a finance class but it strikes me as a bit dry for regular play. The problem is that until new players understand these ideas they will get crushed and after a few games general strategy becomes obvious (e.g. encourage others to promote your doubles in the early rounds). I realize that if you can bring together four highly skilled players it could become a fascinating tactical game but getting to that point is probably not worth the effort (unless you have a homogeneous group that does not mind repetition and rarely introduces new players). There are so many other great games that work from the first play that I rate this game a point lower than it probably deserves.

As an addendum, you will notice from my other ratings that I strongly dislike blind bidding. This game has it on some auctions but it is implemented perfectly such that skilled players are more capable of determing the appropriate amount below expected value to bid.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An auction game of fairly minimalistic design, with a hand management layer and a scoring layer and not much besides. By choosing what cards to auction from your hand, you manipulate expectations, set up opportunities and invite collusion. The theme is oddly appropriate. The uncertain valuation and round ending mechanism are reminiscent of Flinke Pinke, although there's more texture and depth here. A few concerns: 1) The decisions made in the game are highly mathematical in nature and one wrong move could throw the game (especially the fixed price auctions are sensitive to seating order). Calculating the potential value of cards is pretty much the core activity, so stay away if that sounds unengaging. Players also need to be able to focus on not just how much they're making from a purchase, but how much the seller is making as well. 2) The double auctions are powerful and valuable and you simply may not be dealt any. A lot hinges on the third deal: if for instance Krypto's been neglected for the first two rounds and you suddenly hold every last Krypto going into the third round, your hand is simply worth less than everybody else's and you won't make as much money selling and you probably won't win. 3) I'm not at all sure the game wouldn't be better if there were just one or maybe two types of auction (open and hidden).

RATING: 6
Based on 2 plays.  Although certainly not bad, MA is one of my least favorites of Knizia's auction games.  The auction system, whereby the bidder pays to the auctioneer, creates a series of game-theory style dilemmas which are better suited for mathematics than board games.  Once you can calculate with reasonable accuracy (which has not been very difficult in my experience), prices tend to approach X-1, where X is the projected value.

RATING: 8
初の競りゲー。なかなかおもろい。
勝ち筋はいかに安く買うかではなく、いかに高く相手に売りつけるかにあるような気がする。ダブルがかなり強い。カードを売る機会が単純に増えるから。あるだけ使った方がいいかもしれん。カードを買うかどうかの基準はカードを出した人との持ち金の差が重要。
もしみんなが同点の場合、出されたカードの期待値の半額が適正値。そのカードの期待値をどう見積もるかを争うゲーム。自分に高く売れそうなカードが手元にあれば適正値より高く売ってもいいかも。しかしこんなに買い叩くゲームになるとは思わへんかった。あとはカードマネジメント。高く売ることができそうなデザイナーとかダブルとかをいいタイミングで吐き出せるか。１ラウンド目にリードを奪えるとその後の試合展開がやや楽。

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Let's see . . . a constant stream of tense , exciting decisions of risk and investment.  Five kinds of auctions means it never gets repetitive.  The season-to-season value rollover means the game gets more and more dramatic as you progress.  And it has a brilliantly satirical theme, one that only gets better if you ham it up a bit and give names to paintings and biographies to artists. 

Modern Art is a winner on all counts.  This is not a game of number-crunching.  It's a game of relationships -- of creating situations in which the situation that is most beneficial to your opponent is MORE beneficial to you.  Brilliant.  One of the most original designs I've seen.  

Is there a flaw?  Well, a new player, or someone who just can't grasp certain basic concepts in the game, can completely unbalance it for everyone else, causing windfalls for undeserving plays or playing random paintings that throw the whole economy out of whack and render good play and forward-thinking moot.  I tend to prefer games that punish a bad player by himself, not the group as a whole.  

But in a group of players who think ahead and stay on their toes, this is absolutely one of the most exciting games out there.  Margins of victory can be extremely close:  I just took second in my last game, 404k to 407!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.8
An excellent game of art speculation and auctions.  Despite the convoluted math analysis on this site, it’s really much more a game of psychology than of math — If you can sell your paintings toward the end of their most highly-priced rounds, you’ll do very well.  So try to figure out who is pushing which artists, and you can time your sales to take advantage of their plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Rating based on 3 3-player games.  I fully expect this rating to rise to a 9 as soon as I get to play with more players, since it was readily apparent how more players would add to the richness and depth of this game.  Probably will end up as a 10 with enough time and plays.

This game is just really fun.

UPDATE: I've now played this with 4, and it is MUCH better (as I expected) so I've bumped it to 8.5.  Still waiting for a chance to play with 5 though.

UPDATE: Still no 5p plays yet (*sigh*), but this game is really growing on me with more plays.  As the strategic possibilities are beginning to reveal themselves the game is becoming much richer and more engaging than it was as an almost purely tactical exercise. Modern Art is starting to seep into my thoughts even when I'm not actually playing it, and is the first serious rival I've found for Princes of Florence as my favourite Euro.  I dunno, maybe I'm just an auction whore :)

UPDATE: i now have a couple of 5p games under my belt, and it really is great. My fellow players have yet to get beyond the rudiments of strategy, however (my main competitor tends to play it as a "headology" game as opposed to my strategic approach), which means that it hasn't had a chance to shine in its full glory for me yet.

Fingers crossed.

UPDATE: Okay, I've played with 5 a couple of times, plus several other plays with 4. This is a timeless classic, and I will always play it happily. Expected Value barely counts as the tip of the iceberg of the strategy and tactics in this game. Strategic hand management, reading information from your opponents' bids, bluffing with your own bids...and...just...more!

The only fly in the ointment is that, with some players, you have to focus more on what types of mistakes they will make rather than on playing the game to its fullest. In particular, the bandwagon effect, and players who bid erratically can drag it down a little. Even so, it [i]is[/i] possible to adjust, and it is still well worth playing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A great Reiner Knizia auction game where you auction paintings using different auction types.  Once you have 5 of the same painter's art out in round, you score.  Each painter is paid more if they have sold more in the auction round.  The only down side is that players can overpay for a painting.  This can allow someone else to win when you have played well.  

A good fun game and it is a great family weight game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Mayfair edition.  Modern Art is a deep card game with strategic implications that most players don't realize.  The rules are easily teachable but how to create a game plan is not.  The result is that most players will play it once or twice, not get it, and move on to another game.  Like other sophisticated auction games where players are both selling and buying, it's hard for new players to get their heads around the value of a painting.  There is also a poker/psychological element to the game--bluffing and trying to manipulate other players to join with you in playing particular paintings to the table.  The best players always win this game.

RATING: 4
12/02/03 Played a four-player game. I won but I have no idea how.
09/20/05 Played another four-player game. The heart of the game, figuring out what the works of art are worth relative to each other (so you know what to bid) was quite baffling for me. If it hadn't been for one player (who was an economics professor) giving me constant advice on the probable worth of items up for auction, I'd have had no idea what to bid. 

I have a strange reaction to Modern Art. Its more complicated than I'd prefer for an auction game. I sort of like it, but I'm absolutely no good at it. I just can't grasp the relative worth of the items. So it turns into an exercise in frustration. I might play it again... sometime.

RATING: 6
Mayfair Games edition.

Not only is this not nearly as exciting as Ra - the mathematics behind it make it more cold and calculating and a ton less intuitive. The more I've played this, the more irritated I become. I feel I am constantly at the whim of the stupidity of the other people at the table. Maybe it's my own fault for trying this several times with non-gamers, but I think this is a game that people think is deep, but turns out to be nothing more than a trump and bid card game. Blegh. I would trade it away if I thought my wife wouldn't yell at me (she actually likes it.)

Furthermore, the Mayfair edition I have has fairly lousy production quality - cards with black borders that I have to handle carefully (or spend money to put in protectors) or the wear will eventually show around the edges, and the art itself is UGLY. I get that the ugly art may itself be a commentary on modern art, but in this case it literally looks like some 8th grade graphic design class slapped it together.

Sublime. This might be the most brilliant design I have encountered. Extremely accessible but always interesting for gamers. The theme IS the game; I've never seen the two as perfectly melded as they are here. RATING: N/A
This game is in a different class.
It's a simple card game (like Trendy) but each card played triggers an auction to buy the card. 
There are 4 different types of auction as well as a double play card.

When you get into it the game is mostly about bluffing.

I teach the game with the use of the rule where a single double card is passed around the table and anyone who can add an auction card to it takes all the spoils. 
This is called the Double Trouble variant in the rules.
Try it!!
