RATING: 10
One of Knizia's best games.  Game balance can be thrown off if all players are not actively computing the amount they stand to make versus the amount the opponent stands to make for any given transaction.  Good play requires ability to do simple math and make high pressure sales.  Holding cards that are in high demand also comes in very handy, but you have some control in that regard by holding the best cards until round three.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Modern Art is frequently mentioned among Knizia's best auction games and rightfully so. The idea is simple: bid on items where the value is set by the players' own expectations and nothing else. Take turns to choose a painting to auction. The more an artist is sold, the higher the value of the paintings. The fact that values are accumulated over seasons adds an important but difficult timing challenge as well.

What speaks against Modern Art is that group thinking plays an important part in the game. Auction paintings randomly and you'll get a random and most likely boring game. I've only scratched the surface of this little gem but I expect it to offer a lot with the right group.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Fairly enjoyable auction game, but I really dislike the abruptness with which a player can end a round when he or she is the only one who benefits from it.  Also, getting dealt lots of double auction cards without matches for them seriously hurts your ability to make money off your opponents.  I strongly recommend a house rule stating that a player who gets dealt more than X (you decide) double auction cards at the beginning of the game can demand a redeal.

RATING: 10
After multiple plays I can better see what people are raving about.  At first the game seems like a dull mathematical exercise.  Then you realize that the math is really all relative to each players' standing - so it's really a rather complex multi-faceted mathematical exercise.  Not to mention the psychology of riding the fads and trying to read what others have in their hands.

Solid.

---update--

Even better than solid.  The ups and downs this game can draw out of the participants during play solidifies Modern Art's place in the highest tier.

--update--
...or rather, reaffirmation.  After playing twice consecutively last night in which the two games had very different flows I have solidified Modern Art's place amongst my highest rated games.  Must must must have!

--update--

OMG this game is amazing with 3!  Every auction is of critical importance.  It's crazy how a game that can feel almost casual with 4 turns into a brain-burner when you remove one player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a simple and fun interactive economic game that plays best with the maximum number of players (5).  Players take turns auctioning art to the other players.  Then at the end of each round, the art from the most popular artists can be sold back to bank for a profit, whereas the art from the other artists become worthless.  The game is a balance of selling art for profit, and trying to collect art for even bigger profits.

The game comes with screens so that you can hide the amount of money that you are collecting for the winner of the game is the player with the most money.  I don't like games that use screens, and generally I avoid games that have them, but I don't mind the screens in this game.

RATING: 8
心得:
豪哥家玩得
很需要精算的遊戲
當然人多也需要嘴砲哄抬價格
挺燒腦的
規則:
*每位玩家100一百元，起始手牌3/4/5人時11/9/8張，錢用屏風遮住，手上的牌隱密。
*隨機起始，順時針輪流賣，手牌一定要選一張賣，由他自己來主持，放買到的玩家前面，順時針賣下去，直到任一畫家的畫出現了五張即結束(因為Double 出現也一樣)，第五張不拍賣
*每一張畫都有指定的拍賣方式，下圖玩家手上的卡片拍賣方式從左到右依序為
秘密喊價、定價、公開喊價、公開喊價、一回合競標、定價、成套賣畫
>Open -- 公開競價
任何人都能自由喊價，手上也必需有那麼多錢，主持人也能參與，沒有人喊價，主持人零元拿，
賣畫的錢歸主持人所有，若是主持人買則將錢給銀行，這一條規則也適用於Round, Sealed 和Fixed 
>Round (Once around) -- 一回合競標
由主持人左邊的人開始喊價，順時針繞一圈，可以選擇棄權，也可以喊一個價，主持人為最後一個喊價
>Sealed -- 秘密喊價（暗標）
每一個人(包括主持人)從暗中拿錢在手上同時亮出，如果最高價平手，則由主持人左手邊算下去最近的那一個人得標，除非主持人也出最高價，則由主持人得標。
>Fixed -- 定價
主持人出一個定價，這個價必須低於主持人手上的錢，順時針問有沒有人要買，都沒有人接受必須自己買下
>Double -- 成套賣畫
Double的畫有兩種賣法:
一）主持人拿出一張Double，再配上另一張同畫家但非Double的畫作，兩張一起賣。
    	賣法則依第二張的種類。
二）主持人拿出一張Double，從左邊按順序願不願意參加，必須打出一張同畫家但非Double的畫作，如果一圈下來都沒人願意的話，則主持人能免費得到那一張畫，否則參加人變成新的主持人，以第二張畫的賣法賣畫，賣得的錢由對分，奇數的話，多出的一元由新的主持人得到，如果是舊的主持人買下則付一半給行、一半給新主持人，奇數多出的一元給新主持人，新主持人買下的話也是一樣，只是要把奇數多出的一元給銀行
***注意：兩人合作賣出兩張畫後，下一個由新主持人左邊的人開始賣畫，中間的人全被跳過！
*回合結束 -- 結算
回合結束後開始結算畫價，按出現畫的數目(平手左邊贏)放30/20/10元籌碼放在對應回合數上，標碼就是畫的價值，所有的面前畫依標碼的價格賣給銀行，移除遊戲(沒錢的也是)
*下一回合 先補牌，3/4/5人補6/4/3張，第四回合不補，由上一回合結束遊戲者的左邊那一個人先開始
*第二回合之後，如果畫家這一回合擠入前三名，前面回合的畫價累計
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Modern Art's been played a bit, 13 times. You don't have a choice about who to sell to. The auction dictates. So here the trash talk is fun in bidding your wares up, especially if you throw in some imagination in describing the paintings. But it's also a game I don't rate highly, reason following. The aim is to get the value up in the cards you hold most of and sell for max dollars (play your doublers) when the value is highest. But you only have one fifth (fourth, third) opportunity at directing long term value in the artists you're holding. You're pretty much at the whim of the other plays as to where and when the artists you want get driven up, and that largely depends on what people hold most of (around the table) in the first (and second) round, as they'll set the first benchmarks. So the more we played this, the more we became able to correctly value potential value (based on player psychology, cards played, ownership so far, etc), then the more that chance became a determinant in the final round when values were highest - did you have more cards for the highest valued artists in your hand than the other players. And if you sold one less card than other players in the final round, the lost $40k can be game determining, as well as if your doubler was in a weak artist due to the vagaries of the play. You could count money along the way and bid accordingly, but I know the other players aren't. So, whilst fun to play, we've moved on from this as well, apart from the occasional nostalgia play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I do see the point many respected reviewers make when saying the poor play of a player can ruin the game or perhaps hand the game to someone.  I can also see that poor card draws can be a factor.  But none of that bothers me much.  There are some variants that make sure each player has access to his/her fair share of double auction cards so the card draw factor can be mitigated.  And as to the newbie player ruining the game, if you are playing with newbies it should be about having fun more than winning.  Once everyone is up to speed, the personalities of the players contributes as much to the game as the rules do and this makes the game replayable and better than many other auction/bidding games.  There is a lot of skill in picking the correct auction at the correct time and so hand management, another of my favorite game mechanisms, can play a huge role.  You may take a loss on a painting just to set up opportunity for a future round.  This is simply a classic euro that is able to get out of the way of the players.  

And a further note: the new CMON reprint is one of the best reprints I have ever seen. You could spend hours just looking at the cards.

RATING: 10
I first bought this in 2008 and rated it a 10. In 2015 it is still a 10.  It is a hit in my family, and with dedicated gamers also.  For a mere deck of cards and bag of chips, strategy is subtle and deep.  The design of this game design is straightforward: on your turn you select a card from your hand and auction it to the other players.  I think the reason for its amazing success is that there is a lot of luck here, but the luck is not so obvious to see because the auctions dominate the game. Winning requires good choices in the auctions, and also assessing the probability of drawing favorable cards.

Thoughts on strategy and tactics: the tactical game is to try to make the most money by selling paintings when its your turn.

The strategic game only became apparent to me after many plays: observe and manipulate other players' incentives. Launch artists in the early rounds with doubles. With good strategy you can end up with a hand of very valuable cards in the 3rd and 4th rounds that players will bid aggressively for. There is a powerful zero-sum pattern here too: your opponents might find the cards they hold to be less valuable than yours. If you have saved open-auction cards of the highly placed artists for the 3rd and 4th rounds, you have a good chance of winning.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Modern Art is an auction-lover's auction game.  This card-based game contains a variety of different auction types, including once-around auctions, free-form bidding auctions, blind in-the-fist auctions, and fixed "I name the price and the first person who accepts gets it" auctions.  Unlike other auction games, you can reasonably deduce what things will actually be worth, though your hand of cards gives you a little extra information, especially if you are soon in the turn order.  Like many auction games with auctioneers, this one can be a bit fragile if an inexperienced player cedes too much value to the auctioneer.  Also, the "double" cards are inherently more powerful, and getting a disproportionate number of them can put a player at an advantage.  Still, this is an enjoyable game.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 9
Mordern Art is a classic in every sense.  You spend the first few plays kind of lost why who won what and why and after you get past that, you move onto trying to understand everyone's motives and how manipulate the market.  This game would be better if a strategy guide was included with the game.  Not anything super long but much is lost if people don't know how to valuate paintings and manipulate the market.  For that reason, it fires on all cylinders in a competitive group but misfires and can bungled with people that don't fully understand the game and put in bad bids at auction. The winner is the person that understands the group dynamics and sees how to best make his paintings the most valuable and knows which paintings are undervalued at auction.   I won games where I buy almost everything and games where I buy almost nothing.  The game really shines due to group dynamics and all its flaws can be due to group dynamics.  The only thing outside of the play that people might criticize is that it isn't an overtly social  game like a deduction game like Werewolf or Avalon.  It is a tense, competitive game that will provide a few laughs over the bad modern art and a few more when someone gets screwed and all their paintings become worthless.  Great auction game if you are into auctions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Time to change my comments about this strange little, "Modern Art" game.  In a nutshell - it's my favourite auction game.  It takes a bit of explaining to understand some of the nuances of the game, but once it's grasped it can be a heck of a lot of fun.  My only caveat with the game is: noone is allowed to spend more on a painting than it's 'maximum possible value' - as to help curb any possible kingmaking.  Last game I played was with 6, I was the only guy who knew how to play, but everyone who joined in found it to be an excellent time.  A very good game, for a very reasonable cost, in a small box. 

As a side note:  this is not much like For Sale! at all.  There is definately more thinking involved, as players have direct influence over the values of the cards in the deck - in regards to what is going to be auctioned off and how much it will be 'worth' at the same time.  It is [i]that[/i] key difference that makes this the real champion of 'auction' games, with plenty of exciting table-talk.

RATING: 9
I was resistant to the idea of playing a Reiner Knizia game for a while, because someone so prolific and popular couldn't possibly be that good. Then I played Modern Art, and it was a sublime wonderment to experience. While I've had some hit or miss experiences with some of his games (ones which common consensus has damned to history), it seems that his bigger hits are pure objects of game design.

If more paintings by Gitter sell, Gitter paintings can be sold for more. The more they sell, the more you buy, and the more you sell them for. An auction game where the prices are driven up by demand in a way that is so easy to understand that the game is just brilliant. 

The European language 2009 edition has beautiful graphic design and art that really makes the game objet d'art.

RATING: 8
Auction games are the generally difficult to get started. It is hard to value the bids, It usually takes a few plays to asses the value.  
Modern Art has several things going for it. The value of the auction is relatively apparent by watching the purchase chits, and yes you can game the other players by cashing out on item, but the range of possibilities are there.
I like the multiple kinds of auctions - it provides for variety. 
Finally the game is thematic - you do feel like you are trying to convert an unknown (and pretty bad one at that) artist into a highly desirable one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Was quite impressed by first play of this. Played with CMON's newest version. 

This is a smart auctioning game that plays a little oddly. At first most of us assumed that like most games the main way to get money would be to buy things in the auctions that would later sell for more. And while that is one way to get money, the math suggests the real way to win this game is to be putting up for auction the most valuable art each round. And that leads to a very interesting game of trying to use your hand of art to put forth the artwork that will set up huge sales in later rounds, netting you the most profit. 

Of course a bit of profit can still be made through all the various auctions, and because of the round to round scoring it can be hard to know what the art will sell for exactly. Everyone's bids start to reveal little bits of information they know about their hand which starts to influence how the game will play out. 

Ultimately what I'm trying to say is that with relatively few rules the game is pretty deep and quite open to mathematical analysis which are features I like. There is plenty of player interaction with the others in the auctions and it leads to a fun time. This is a game I'll happily play some more. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
UPDATE Feb 2011, finally played a full game of this; I rated this game last in Feb 2006.

Downgrade from 6 to 4.

It's a refreshing change to play a Euro that's not a bloated mess that we have today, caused by the inexplicable popularity of worker placement games.

Still, this game is very meh.

It's a game that rewards the person who's willing to put the time to calculate the value of what each card is, which is quite possible in Modern Art. So I suppose fans of calculation intensive yet low interaction games like Power Grid are also big fans of Modern Art.

I say low interaction because it's really all about trying to put a numerical object on a value. There's no mechanism to manipulate how opponents will play; you simply have to predict how they will behave. That might be a valid and interesting form of interaction; I find it bland anti-social.

There is some tension in hoping that an opponent doesn't suddenly end a round by playing the first fifth card of an artist, but usually that's a matter of luck; the player who holds the most equals sign cards (and knows how to use them properly) usually does the best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7
Players: 3-5
Time: 45 min.

Five different artists have produced a bunch of paintings, and it's the player's task to be both the buyer and the seller, hopefully making a profit in both roles. You do this by putting a painting from your hand up for auction each turn. You get the money if some other player buys it, but must pay the bank if you buy it for yourself. After each round, paintings are valued by the number of paintings from that artist that were sold. The broker with the most cash after four rounds is the winner. A clever game of market speculation and manipulation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Modern art is a beautifully stripped down game of the free market.  Two parties exchange goods for the betterment of both parties.  If you think a game centered solely on mutual benefit would make a lousy competitive game, you're probably right.  So Knizia throws in two beautiful, simple curves into the equation:  1.  The value of paintings in the future is variable and depends solely on the market transactions people choose to make.  2.  Different auction methods allow people to determine a certain bias as to who will win the painting cheaply, or who will need to spend a lot to get a painting.  

What all this boils down to is a game that involves thinking about everyone else more than yourself.  You must decide, from a very analytical view, what people want, what they are willing to do for it, and how you can use their goals to your own advantage.  It's a beautiful system of cooperation for maximum private gain.

RATING: 7
(+) Acheives freeform auction in purest form.  
(+) Brilliant elements of card play that can add twists to valuation of art.  
(+) High player interaction and demands keen attention from all players involved.  

(-) Strategic choices are oddly, consistently opaque. It feels rather tricky to play correctly heuristically, which is something I have come to enjoy from most typical purdue auction games.
(-) Pure auction and nothing else, and so the repetitive nature of the turn structure can be a turn off.  
(-) Publishing effort is a mixed bag. Good card art, unusually fiddly choice of currencies.

RATING: 10
Woooooaaaaahhhhhhhhhh.... It's important to understand that this game is not just about playing the numbers, but about manipulating the whole game-flow. 

You can play by the numbers, or you can push around the market and make a big score by selling at the end.

The majesty of this design blows my mind. Imagine what it would be like without the cumulative build of scoring from round to round, or what it would be if you didn't get most of your cards up front. 

NOTE: years later, I'm still playing this, still loving this, and I dig it so much that it's the centerpiece for a paper I'm writing about game aesthetics and perfect game design.

RATING: 9.1
This is a very curious and very successful auction board game. For us, its theme doesn´t matter (as usual in Reiner Knizia´s games...), although this game is OK in this point. Production design is superb, and very brave if we consider the conservative fashion that nowadays prevails when illustrating a board game. So far, it's the best auction board game I've ever tried. It's a lot of fun, and there´s certain strategy involved. We´ve mainly played with 4 players, and we love it. Moreover, this new edition has excellent quality for its price (CMON have done a very good job here!), and as I said before, this edition of this old game is beautiful. If you like auctions and Contemporary Art, it´s highly recommended!

RATING: 4
I can see why people consider it the essential auction game, but I don't find auction games particularly interesting where it is so easy to directly correlate what you are spending on something and what you expect to get in return. Knowledge of what's in your hand creates some different expectations about how much a color will be worth, but essentially cards are not of different value to different players.  I prefer auction games where players vary in what value they get out of what they're bidding on, and that that value is not that same thing they are bidding (unlike money for money, or points for points).  Thematically, the art is just really boring and it doesn't feel like we are bidding on art at all, so I would just as well have it completely abstract like Coloretto.

RATING: 6
Under normal circumstances, I would rate this game a 6.  The theme is so-so and whenever I play, I get a sense that my success in the game has very little to do with how I play.  Instead, it all depends on what your opponents do.  Obviously, by recognizing this, my strategy should change from game to game (or opponent to opponent), however I can not figure out that winning formula.  This confusion/frustration intrigues me, keeps me coming back, and gives Modern Art an extra bump up to a 7 out of 10. 

Another point - I know of a few gamer friends who absolutely love this game.  This causes me to search for the greatness in this game that I enevitably can't see.

The way you appraise the art is neat.  Lack of a $10,000 denomination chip is annoying.

RATING: 10
Like Lost Cities, Lord of the Rings, and Tigris & Euphrates, Modern Art is a game where my first experience was not indicative of the games brilliance. I originally worried this was too fragile, allowing enthusiastic bidders to throw the game by pumping too much money toward the other players. I've shifted on this in two directions. First of all, I no longer need a game to require the players to play well. Secondly, that fragility works in the game's favor thematically, where falling for the hype only helps your opponents. This is a brilliant bidding game, highly thematic and very nuanced. It's one of Knizia's best.

The new edition from CMON is so much better than the last Mayfair edition that it's a little silly. It's one of my favorite board game productions out there right now.

RATING: 8


Owned and enjoyed for many years, then sold because I was an idiot in a bad mood. Recently paid $$$ to get the pictured 1996 version that I am used to back in my collection. One good thing about capping your collection, games that you like go up in personal value--that is, it is easier to justify spending a lot of money to get a game into your collection. You only have so many, so there's no danger of spiraling out of control. I would pay $100 for this version, easy.

don't know why I like it so much--we like to pretend that we are snobby art dealers, pawning this crap off on the other unsuspecting players. Of course, we're as likely to hose ourselves as them if we're not careful.

Love the pictures, pretending I knew Crypto's mother, which 'explains things' if you know what I mean.

My fandom's a mystery to me! The good Dr. Knizia does it again, confusing my understanding of my own preferences!

RATING: 9
All auctions, all the time. A staggering work of heartbreaking genius. Mayfair version is ugly as sin and an affront to this fine game.

There's a rules change between the German and English versions:

"The Mayfair version of the game published in the United States modified the German rules so that when a player completes a double auction card played by another player, the sale price is divided between each player who played a card. The original German rules (which I prefer) have the entirety of the sale price going to the player who plays the SECOND card."

RATING: 9
Auction games are difficult (especially for new players) because it's hard to figure what things are worth in the context of the scoring.  But this game presents this problem in a very unique way - instead of the items bid on having a fixed value (like say Ra or Medici), the players' choices in Modern Art are what determine the value of the items they've bid on.  And I find it so hard to wrap your head around how paying for a painting affects your position in the game.  The best auction game around, in my opinion.

RATING: 8
Sell your paintings for maximum gain, buy paintings you think will become valuable (but don't spend too much unless you're cooperating with the seller), and use the double paintings wisely.

After my 2nd play (and this time with experienced [floatleft][imageid=536712][/floatleft]opponents), I can see why this is so popular. It helped that I also took a class on auction theory in between my 1st and 2nd plays. Very computational, and you can even develop implicit alliances in the game. I like it, though it might be too opaque and unintuitive for non-gamers or new players. I prefer teaching Ra to newbies because its intuitive auctions are easy to teach, but will play this anytime because the mechanism-heavy auctions offer something entirely different. A gamers' game.

I printed out my homemade deck featuring real artwork through Artscow. Bittersweet memories: it kept me occupied after my mind-blowing trip to Europe (and its art museums), when I came back to the reality of unemployment in a hostile time for new graduates..

RATING: 8
Scandinavian edition.

The ultimate auction game.  Nicely designed.  We started off loving this game (4 of us played several sessions), then decided that the strategy was too predictable, took a break from it, came back and re-discovered the surprisingly deep complexity.  I often find myself winning at the beginning of the last round, only to blow it when the paintings start selling for massive amounts.  Requires more planning and thinking than you might expect.  A great game for any collection (and easy to teach to non-gamers!).

The Scandinavian edition features "real" art by 4 artists.  Some of the art is quite good, some is horrendous, but the overall production value of this version is quite high.  

5-4-14 (update):  I am upping my rating of this game to an 8.0.  After years on my shelf and numerous plays with different groups, I've decided it's just a genius design that keeps on giving.  There is so much strategy here, so much social psychology, so much intensity.  Love it.

RATING: 10
Considering how much I disliked Ra (edit: initially) and have overall tendencies of avoiding Knizia's games whenever possible, it surprised me how much I enjoyed Modern Art. And I won against eurogaming crowd, who were lost in the groupthink, mwahaha. (Prices went up in speculative bubble and I just sold and rarely bought.) I love groupthink games as it makes it harder to evaluate and make optimal moves (well, you can, but each time dependant on groupthink). It reminded me of stock market games (stock being cards you have in your hand) which have similar dynamics, but this one is lighter and easier to learn. The logic behind it seems a bit similar to Genoa, though here I managed to win by not going into bidding wars and exploiting the groupthink instead of just riding with it. The pegasus edition art is lovely and helps get the theme come across and getting players in the right mood. My GF loved it as well, and as we do work in the field of art, this looks like something I'll acquire my own copy of very soon.

RATING: 9
The way in which the game evokes the speculative (and frankly nearly fraudulent) nature of modern art dealing is a masterpiece in Euro game design. The real modern art used on the cards greatly enhances the theme. Basically, the theme is perfectly baked into the game and it's done in a very simple and intuitive way. It's fascinating how group psychology can dictate the value of paintings. One small issue I have is that a lot can depend on drawing the double-auction paintings which can be worth a lot in the later rounds -- relatively short playtime does mitigate this.

RATING: 8
Modern Art is a pure auction game that asks one simple question:  how much is that worth to you?  Each player on his turn places a work of art from his hand up for sale in an auction format.  The key is in estimating the future worth of the painting (at the end of the round).  A round ends when five paintings by the same artist have been put up for auction, so a round could be very short or very long (there are five artists).  The paintings that were purchased are all sold to the bank at the end of each round.  Paintings that are by the most popular artist (for that round) get a $30G pricetag, 2nd most popular gets $20G and 3rd gets $10G.  All others are worthless.
I like the tension of the auctions, particularly the open auctions that see players drop out early and jump back in at higher prices.  There's a fair bit of psychology to the game, especially as the seller.  You often put works up for sale that you want yourself, but not for too high a price.  As the buyer, you have to be aware that you're putting money into an opponents coffers when you win an auction, so that should inform your decision.  All in all, it's a great game that can generate a lot of laughs and BS with the right group.  Unreservedly recommended to anyone who doesn't hate auctions.

RATING: 9
This is the most elegant and amazing auction game, and it will always be in my top 5.  There's a variety of different auctions available, so you get great variety.  Since you get to choose on your turn what to auction and make the proceeds from it - and the set of paintings already sold in the round affects how much they are actually worth - there is a vast variety of interesting decisions on your turn.  Even when bidding on other people's paintings, it's interesting to figure out if it's worth trying to buy something not yet sold on the cheap, or to pay through the nose for a work that has an artist who has already had a couple of other paintings sold.  The rules are quick and easy, and this is simply a great, great game.

RATING: 8
Probably the deepest pure auction game there is. The game is just a basic structure around which players create a market. Players win by manipulating market incentives to their advantage. Nothing has intrinsic value, and the market can change quickly, so it's easy to get caught up in things and overspend, as befits a game about modern art. The rules are simple, but the game is subtle and deep and interesting, and the components of the French edition are excellent. 

Critics say that the game is fragile to inexperience because one bad choice can swing the game. This is true in theory but not true of my experience. I have rarely seen players blunder too badly, at least by comparison to any other game that rewards experience. Experienced players almost always win. But I always explain the minimum and maximum value any painting may have at the beginning of each round. I emphasize in the rules explanation that you do not have to buy a painting to win the game. Intelligent opponents catch on quickly, typically too late to win, but they ask to play again. It is one of Knizia's best designs, and it's the most engrossing of his auction games, though it's not as exciting as Ra.

RATING: 8.5
The mechanics of this game are so elegant and appropriate, it is truly a thing of beauty. Get four or five people who know what they're doing and this could be your new all-time favorite. Fortunately the game is so great that it can afford to be hideous, perhaps the next edition will have more atmosphere. I'm super pissed the new ed. changed the game play. How awful.

RATING: 6.5
The biggest compliment that I can give this game is that a friend of mine, who is a self-proclaimed board game hater, played this game once with a group of buddies and promptly went out and purchased a copy.  The biggest complaint I have with this game is that, for a game about "modern art", the visual graphics are TERRIBLE (better bits could have increased my "game design" rating by a full point).  But over-all, it is a fun game and is the most interesting auction-based game that I've played due to the variety of different auction styles that are used.  I find it to be more fun and interesting than "Ra", anyhow.  And it's got some very interesting strategic depth below the surface.  I recently played a 4-player game where I purchased the vast majority of the paintings (it was quite the art monopoly!) and everyone was convinced that I had won by a long shot (including myself), but I ended up in third place, losing out to a guy who purchased only a couple of paintings but managed to sell his for quite high prices (and he beat the second place player by only a few points).  So I'm still a little puzzled by the strategy, which is good!  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 6.5/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Sublime auction game with a role-playing element. What I find most enjoyable is the problem, as auctioneer, of setting the maximum value at which a painting will be bought by someone else -- i.e., because it is usually more profitable to sell a painting worth 10 for 6 (gain for other plyer 4; for you, 6) than buy it yourself for 7 (gain for you, 3).

Like many auction games, it suffers somewhat with inexperienced players who under or overpay; more seriously, there is a strong turn-order binding problem where these players set fixed prices too low and help the player to the left.

It's a pity that the graphic design is so poor and, as I have come to expect from Mayfair, so are the components.

RATING: 7.5
[Mayfair 2004 edition] {Arts; auction, set collection; optimal 4p} A typical Knizia's auction game with many calculations. MA has rich player interaction and loads of fun. MA is barely luck dependent; actually I think it's a medium-weight strategy game, in which players to find a way to play the auction cards effectively to earn big money.

Even though MA involves continual calculations, general players can still master it well. However, all auction games share a similar problem: inexperienced players will spend too much in bidding and weaken themselves; as a result, riches get richer. It's even critical in MA because a player has to master many ways of auction and plan how to develop the masterpieces of an artist. Inexperienced will easily lose their mind in this game.

I have a handful of Knizia's auction games - High Society, Money!, MvS, Traumfabrik, Ra, Razzia!, previous Palazzo and this one. I can't decide which one is the best because each game is special and unique. However, I like them all! (should I get Medici, too? :devil: )

It's a game about ark work collection but the ark work of this edition is just horrible! Nicer card graphic will definitely make me love it more.  The missing of $10 chips is very troublesome.  The player screens are just too thin.  And the rulebook is of very low quality (like a photocopy with misaligned cutting).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This was way more fun than I thought it would be. Players have hidden money and can do types of bids on cards - hidden bid, fixed bid, general auction bit, or a bid around the table depending on the card. The artist that sells the first second or third most paintings in a round gets to sell those paintings and then the next round the 30 20 10 score carries over but only if that artist succeeds again. Be careful in giving too much money to other players or overbidding on a painting that won't get you enough profit.

RATING: 7
Accomplished card-based auction game. The aim is to collect artworks from various fictional artists when they appear at auction. There are several different types of auction e.g. fixed price, hidden bid, open bid etc, and this mixture of mechanics brings a level of variety to the game that is absent from other auction games. The theme actually works well and for a game as simple as this, it mirrors the dealings of the art world really well as the popularity of particular artists fluctuates wildly and the dealer who best controls the market will be the most successful.

RATING: 8
My first game of Dr K. 

Once players understand the types of auctions in the game and going through a round to really see the potential of the manipulation of the market, the game really just shines.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
tl;dr: interesting pure auction game

There was a time when Reiner had auction game diarrhea. I just put out game after game after game with all kinds of different auction mechanisms. Most of those have just one auction mechanism, this game features five of them.
Each turn you will auction off one of the active player's cards. The card indicates what type of auction it is.
The system where the value of cards is dependent on the amount that is played and the amount scored in previous rounds, creates a very interesting dynamic.
I must say this game still works surprisingly well and certainly is one of the better pure auction games I know.
Too bad every edition of this game looks like shit!

Initial rating: 7.5/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 6
Interesting game, but to my mind this ranks far behind other Knizia bidding games such as Ra, Traumfabrik and Amun Re. Luck of the card draw (tough to auction off valuable art if you are dealt only types not in vogue, plus the double-auction cards are overpowered and, again, it's completely random who benifits from getting them) and the vagaries of group-think (without a double-auction card, it's impossible to make a line of art valuable unless by someone else's whim) keep this from being a top-tier auction game in my book. The five different auction styles also can be a bit fiddly and doesn't help the cause. All that said, this is still a very solid design and one I'm rarely unwilling to play.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 3
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 2
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 2.75

RATING: 9
(NH)
Letter Grade: A-.  There isn't a more thorough auction game, or one that uses the actions of its players to develop as complex an in-game economy (except, perhaps, Medici)

Excellent fun.  It's easy to teach to new players, though they'll likely not see vistas of strategies open before them until after one or two seasons of scoring.  Plays quickly enough, as well, making it easy to fit into a night of gaming. 
UPDATE: Bumped this up to a 9 after repeated plays.  I've introduced this to non-gamers several times, and it's gone over quite well.  It's a bit hard to sell during the first round, as new players feel a bit anxious about how much to bid on a painting.  But after that it's smooth as silk, easy to introduce and teach.  And hey, I've even won a game of this one...

RATING: 8
Modern Art has a lot going for it. Like most Knizia games, it's extremely streamlined, pairing back all the fluff to focus on a sharp central mechanic.

The bidding is varied and in a single play I was amazed by the different strategies I could employ by offering different artists and different auction types at different times. The market manipulation causes some real stress as you worry about an opponent ending the round before you can position your works properly.

The game itself is super tight and it certainly helps that this new CMON edition of the game is drop-dead gorgeous. Oversize cards with varied, appealing artwork and an overall great aesthetic.

We'll see if this hits the table more often than Ra.

RATING: 8

It's easy to make mistakes that benefit a particular player (which is bad), so the game plays best with players of mostly equal skill. It also surprisingly suffers from the alpha player syndrome, despite being competitive. It encourages any rude player to try and boss you as a mistake you might make, may benefit a third player.

RATING: 8

RATING: 10
Who says auction is a game mechanism.  Auction IS the game.  Speculation and manipulation is great.  I mean how many games have two "ation"'s.  Especially if you get good, then there is domination.  Its only flaw is new players can drastically effect the outcome of this game.  New players are  tougher to manipulate, and make crazy decisions that just make you shake your head.  That being said, new players tend to make similar mistakes, so it can be accounted for. I can't see this game ever getting old for me. 

 It will always be a solid standby. The more I play the more I enjoy it.  Scales well, plays quickly, no two games are the same, high amount of player interaction, very very few hands that are impossible to play and fun.  28 plays and I am still forced to think on my feet. 

 It scratches every gaming itch I have in blissful 45 minutes.  

The hand management is at the core of this game.  Setting up future plays with which artists to play, while setting up future plays by determining which artist to buy.  Sometimes not maximizing expectations in the short run, will maximize expectations in the long run.  The English variant included in the rules is the way I play after trying both out extensively.

RATING: 9
This is an auction game with simple rules. However, the game play is very interesting. Players have to know when and what cards to play in order to maximise the money they earn. With good hand management, players may be able to profit well from selling their art. 

There are various "unspoken" alliances formed during the game. With more players owing a certain art, it will give incentive for the players to play that particular art to increase its value.

I have made 2 new card sets for this edition: Modern Art 1930 using art from five now famous modern artists whose lives intersected in the median of their collective ages in 1930 (Vaserely, Dali, Picasso, Matisse and Escher.) =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In a typical Knizia design, game play is relatively straight forward but player decisions have ripple effects that often cause unintended consequences which are relatively tough to gauge during game play. This is what makes Knizia games brilliant. Case in point, if you own certain art pieces with increasing value in your hand, having it in your own hand is useless. Yet auctioning these pieces away means you won't have them for final valuation unless you buy them yourself which is a double whammy since you won't gain money from selling them. Such is the angst of Modern Art. Lots of interlocking decision making which leads to some tough choices. It is not sufficient to focus on personal optimization but rather react to market forces. Great de
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A game where salesman skills, manipulation, lies and threats are the keys to victory. Very dependant on playgroup as one dumb play by a newbie or a kingmaker will wreck the game.
2x cards are a bit overpowered but have so far not broken our games. A skilled player manipulates the game to go in his preferred direction and those lucky bastards with 2x cards can be countered sometimes.
I've tried playing this without my "original gamegroup" but it sucks so badly I'd rather keep the good memories alive than ruin them further.
To sum it all up: This game lacks in so many parts that it can hardly be considered a good game by todays standards. Falling from a rating 9 to a rating 6.5 just because game groups change is something few games can achieve and point to crappy design choices and very poor balance. However the author of this game is known for just grinding out vast amounts of games without care to finish.

RATING: 9
So freaking tricky.  Short, but still very meaty.  It's usually easy to judge approximately how much a painting will be worth, but there's enough chaos that you don't know for sure.  The different types of auctions and the different payouts for cards as rounds progress means that there's always interesting replay in this one.  I really like how players, with the play of cards, have a limited control on how the market will develop, as well as just being simple buyers and sellers.  Better with fewer players, as the control over the market tightens.  Double auctions have the ability to decide the game, and if one person has significantly more than everybody else, it's their game to lose.

RATING: 8
We also played ' Modern Art', where each player is an auctioneer trying to entice the other auction houses to buy their trendy art whilst trying to predict the art that will generate the most money from sales. That word entice brings back memories of a 1970's 'Busstop expansion! There are a variety of auction actions available, and art is placed first, second and third based on numbers purchased, over four turns so it is the aggregate of these placings - 30 (first) down to 10 (third) - which governs how much could be made in sales that directly influences what bids might be made if the auctioneer is confident of making the proverbial 'killing' Probably best not played with people who are either card sharps and/or who have eidetic memories! An enjoyable game again.

RATING: 8
This is the epitome of auction gaming. I strongly dislike Ra, but find the decisions, interaction and pace of Modern Art to be breath taking. I am always willing to play this, something I can't necessarily say about other "favorites" of mine.[UPDATE 2/23/06] Dropped from 9.5 to 8.0 after two odd plays. While it's still a very good, and perhaps "pure" auction game, there are some odd situations where my only options were to force the end of a round to limit how much money people earned (while earning none myself) or generate nothing by offering a piece with no track record/value built in. Very frustrating to constantly have the players to my right offer up cards I want to auction and setting the threshold to four so my auctions would trigger the end and earn no cash.

RATING: 7
Supremely simple yet rich in depth, Modern Art has a purity which I find very appealing. Unlike many games which force players to consider a number of possible moves, Modern Art focuses everyone&#039;s attention on one auction.  And since the paintings are of equal value to everyone, each player has a stake in every auction.

RATING: 6
There is no doubt in my mind that Modern Art is probably one of the best auction games ever designed, and I do find the variety in the types of bidding available an interesting feature to mask the repetitiveness of the actual gameplay.  However, I just find no joy in something driven purely by this mechanic.  It just seem all too subjective, and also too dependent on what others may or may not do.  There does not appear to be any strategy in speculation, only luck.

RATING: 6
The gameplay is excellent. Modern Art's greatest weakness is, ironically, the art. This game is ugly. Even after playing, once I knew how fun it was, when I looked at the game I wasn't drawn to playing it again.

The system of auctioning paintings off to one another is fantastic. Trying to figure out which artists are going to be valuable each turn can be a bit of a brainburner at times, but successfully catching a painting for less than market value is awesome. Be warned, though: Modern Art can be very mathy, so if you don't like numbers running through your head the entire time you may not like this game.
